<p>The <b>springbok</b> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/ŋ/ 'ng' in 'sing'">ŋ</span><span title="/ˌ/ secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span></span>/</a></span></span> (<i>Antidorcas marsupialis</i>) is a medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a> found mainly in southern and southwestern Africa. The sole member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genus_(biology)" title="Genus (biology)">genus</a> <i>Antidorcas</i>, this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bovid" title="Bovid">bovid</a> was first <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a> by the German zoologist <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_August_Wilhelm_von_Zimmermann" title="Eberhard August Wilhelm von Zimmermann">Eberhard August Wilhelm von Zimmermann</a> in 1780. Three <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> are identified. A slender, long-legged antelope, the springbok reaches 71 to 86 cm (28 to 34 in) at the shoulder and weighs between 27 and 42 kg (60 and 93 lb). Both sexes have a pair of black, 35-to-50-centimetre (14 to 20 in) long <a href="/wiki/Horn_(anatomy)" title="Horn (anatomy)">horns</a> that curve backward. The springbok is characterised by a white face, a dark stripe running from the eyes to the mouth, a light brown <a href="/wiki/Coat_(animal)" title="Coat (animal)">coat</a> marked by a reddish-brown stripe that runs from the upper foreleg to the buttocks across the flanks, and a white <a href="/wiki/Rump_(animal)" title="Rump (animal)">rump</a> flap.</p>
<p>Active mainly at dawn and dusk, springbok form <a href="/wiki/Harem_(zoology)" title="Harem (zoology)">harems</a> (mixed-sex herds). In earlier times, springbok of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalahari_desert" title="Kalahari desert">Kalahari desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Karoo</a> would migrate in large numbers across the countryside, a practice known as <i>trekbokken</i>. A feature unique to the springbok is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronking" title="Pronking">pronking</a>, in which the springbok performs multiple leaps into the air, up to 2 metres (6.6 ft) above the ground, in a stiff-legged posture, with the back bowed and the white flap lifted. Primarily a browser, the springbok feeds on shrubs and <a href="/wiki/Succulent_plant" title="Succulent plant">succulents</a>; this antelope can live without drinking water for years, meeting its requirements through eating succulent vegetation. Breeding takes place year-round, and peaks in the rainy season, when forage is most abundant. A single calf is born after a five- to six-month-long pregnancy; weaning occurs at nearly six months of age, and the calf leaves its mother a few months later.</p>
<p>Springbok inhabit the dry areas of south and southwestern Africa. The International Union for Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>) classifies the springbok as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_Concern" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a> species. There are no major threats to the long-term survival of the species; the springbok, in fact, is one of the few antelope species considered to have an expanding population. They are popular game animals, and are valued for their meat and skin. The springbok is the national animal of <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The common name "springbok" (<small>pronounced </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/ˈspriŋ-ˌbäk/</a></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-MW-3">[3]</a></sup> comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrikaans_Language" title="Afrikaans Language">Afrikaans</a> words <i>spring</i> ("jump") and <i>bok</i> ("<a href="/wiki/Antelope" title="Antelope">antelope</a>" or "<a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a>");<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> the first recorded use of the name dates to 1775. The <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">scientific name</a> of the springbok is <i>Antidorcas marsupialis</i>; <i>anti</i> is <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> for "opposite", and <i>dorcas</i> for "<a href="/wiki/Gazelle" title="Gazelle">gazelle</a>" – identifying that the animal is not a gazelle. The <a href="/wiki/Specific_name_(zoology)" title="Specific name (zoology)">specific epithet</a> <i>marsupialis</i> comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a> <i>marsupium</i> ("pocket"); it refers to a pocket-like skin flap which extends along the midline of the back from the tail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1972_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1972-4">[4]</a></sup> In fact, it is this physical feature that distinguishes the springbok from true gazelles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rafferty_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-rafferty-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The springbok is the sole member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genus_(biology)" title="Genus (biology)">genus</a> <i>Antidorcas</i> and is placed in the <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">family</a> <a href="/wiki/Bovidae" title="Bovidae">Bovidae</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MSW3_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-MSW3-6">[6]</a></sup> It was first <a href="/wiki/Species_description" title="Species description">described</a> by the German zoologist <a href="/wiki/Eberhard_August_Wilhelm_von_Zimmermann" title="Eberhard August Wilhelm von Zimmermann">Eberhard August Wilhelm von Zimmermann</a> in 1780. Zimmermann assigned the genus <i>Antilope</i> (<a href="/wiki/Blackbuck" title="Blackbuck">blackbuck</a>) to the springbok.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1845, Swedish zoologist <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jakob_Sundevall" title="Carl Jakob Sundevall">Carl Jakob Sundevall</a> placed the springbok in <i>Antidorcas</i>, a genus of its own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sundevall_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sundevall-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, Eva Verena Bärmann (of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>) and colleagues undertook a revision of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phylogeny" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tribe_(biology)" title="Tribe (biology)">tribe</a> <a href="/wiki/Antilopini" title="Antilopini">Antilopini</a> on the basis of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleus_(biology)" title="Nucleus (biology)">nuclear</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondrial</a> data. They showed that the springbok and the <a href="/wiki/Gerenuk" title="Gerenuk">gerenuk</a> (<i>Litocranius walleri</i>) form a <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saiga" title="Saiga">saiga</a> (<i>Saiga tatarica</i>) as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_taxon" title="Sister taxon">sister taxon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barmann_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-barmann-9">[9]</a></sup> The study pointed out that the saiga and the springbok could be considerably different from the rest of the antilopines; a 2007 phylogenetic study even suggested that the two form a clade sister to the gerenuk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a> below is based on the 2013 study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barmann_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-barmann-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>

<p><br/>
Fossil springbok are known from the <a href="/wiki/Pliocene" title="Pliocene">Pliocene</a>; the antelope appears to have evolved about three million years ago from a gazelle-like ancestor. Three fossil species of <i>Antidorcas</i> have been identified, in addition to the extant form, and appear to have been widespread across Africa. Two of these, <i>A. bondi</i> and <i>A. australis</i>, became extinct around 7,000 years ago (early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>). The third species, <i>A. recki</i>, probably gave rise to the extant form <i>A. marsupialis</i> during the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>, about 100 thousand years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vrba1973_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vrba1973-11">[11]</a></sup> Fossils have been reported from Pliocene, Pleistocene and Holocene sites in northern, southern and eastern Africa. Fossils dating back to 80 and 100 thousand years ago have been excavated at <a href="/wiki/Herolds_Bay" title="Herolds Bay">Herolds Bay Cave</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Cape_Province" title="Western Cape Province">Western Cape Province</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Florisbad_archaeological_and_paleontological_site" title="Florisbad archaeological and paleontological site">Florisbad</a> (<a href="/wiki/Free_State_(province)" title="Free State (province)">Free State</a>) respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> are recognised:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groves_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-groves-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><i>A. m. angolensis</i> <small>(Blaine, 1922)</small> – Occurs in <a href="/wiki/Benguela" title="Benguela">Benguela</a> and <a href="/wiki/Namibe" title="Namibe">Namibe</a> (southwestern <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>).</li>
<li><i>A. m. hofmeyri</i> <small>(<a href="/wiki/Oldfield_Thomas" title="Oldfield Thomas">Thomas</a>, 1926)</small> – Occurs in <a href="/wiki/Berseba" title="Berseba">Berseba</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Namaqualand" title="Great Namaqualand">Great Namaqualand</a> (southwestern Africa). Its range lies north of the <a href="/wiki/Orange_River" title="Orange River">Orange River</a>, stretching from <a href="/wiki/Upington" title="Upington">Upington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sandfontein" title="Sandfontein">Sandfontein</a> through Botswana to Namibia.</li>
<li><i>A. m. marsupialis</i> <small>(Zimmermann, 1780)</small> – Its range lies south of the Orange River, extending from the northeastern <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">Cape of Good Hope</a> to the Free State and <a href="/wiki/Kimberley,_Northern_Cape" title="Kimberley, Northern Cape">Kimberley</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The springbok is a slender antelope with long legs and neck. Both sexes reach 71–86 centimetres (28–34 in) at the shoulder with a head-and-body length typically between 120 and 150 cm (47 and 59 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> The weights for both sexes range between 27 and 42 kilograms (60 and 93 lb). The tail, 14 to 28 centimetres (5.5 to 11.0 in) long, ends in a short black tuft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nowak_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-nowak-13">[13]</a></sup> There are major differences in the size and weight of the subspecies. A study tabulated average body measurements for the three subspecies. <i>A. m. angolensis</i> males stand 84 cm (33 in) tall at the shoulder, while females are 81 cm (32 in) tall. The males weigh around 31 kg (68 lb) while the slightly heavier females weigh 32 kg (71 lb). <i>A. m. hofmeyri</i> is the largest subspecies; males are nearly 86 cm (34 in) tall, and the notably shorter females are 71 cm (28 in) tall. The males, weighing 42 kg (93 lb), are heavier than females, that weigh 35 kg (77 lb). On the other hand, <i>A. m. marsupialis</i> is the smallest subspecies; males are 75 cm (30 in) tall and females 72 cm (28 in) tall. Average weight of males is 31 kg (68 lb) while for females it is 27 kg (60 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> Another study showed a strong correlation between the availability of winter dietary protein and the body mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-robinson_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-robinson-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dark stripes extend across the white face, from the corner of the eyes to the mouth. A dark patch marks the forehead. In juveniles the stripes and the patch are light brown. The ears, narrow and pointed, measure 15–19 centimetres (5.9–7.5 in). Typically light brown, the springbok has a dark reddish-brown band running horizontally from the upper foreleg to the edge of the buttocks, separating the dark back from the white underbelly. The tail (except the terminal black tuft), buttocks, the insides of the legs and the <a href="/wiki/Rump_(animal)" title="Rump (animal)">rump</a> are all white. There are two other varieties – pure black and pure white <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morph_(zoology)" title="Morph (zoology)">forms</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artificial_selection" title="Artificial selection">artificially selected</a> in some South African ranches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup> Though born with a deep black sheen, adult black springbok are two shades of chocolate-brown and develop a white marking on the face as they mature. White springbok, as the name suggests, are predominantly white with a light tan stripe on the flanks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<table style="background:transparent;margin-top:0.5em;float:right">

<p>The three subspecies also differ in their colour. <i>A. m. angolensis</i> has a brown to tawny coat, with thick dark brown stripes on the face extending two-thirds down to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muzzle_(animal)" title="Muzzle (animal)">snout</a>. While the lateral stripe is nearly black, the stripe on the rump is dark brown. The medium brown forehead patch extends to eye level and is separated from the bright white face by a dark brown border. There is a brown spot on the nose. <i>A. m. hofmeyri</i> is a light fawn, with thin dark brown face stripes. The stripes on the flanks are dark brown to black, and the posterior stripes are moderately brown. The forehead patch, dark brown or fawn, extends beyond the level of the eyes and mixes with the white of the face without any clear barriers. The nose may have a pale smudge. <i>A. m. marsupialis</i> is a rich chestnut brown, with thin light face stripes. The stripe near the rump is well-marked, and that on the flanks is deep brown. The forehead is brown, fawn or white, the patch not extending beyond the eyes and having no sharp boundaries. The nose is white or marked with brown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-groves_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-groves-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>The skin along the middle of the <a href="/wiki/Anatomical_terms_of_location#Dorsal_and_ventral" title="Anatomical terms of location">dorsal</a> side is folded in, and covered with 15 to 20 cm (6 to 8 in) white hair erected by <a href="/wiki/Arrector_pili_muscle" title="Arrector pili muscle">arrector pili muscles</a> (located between <a href="/wiki/Hair_follicle" title="Hair follicle">hair follicles</a>). This white hair is almost fully concealed by the surrounding brown hairs until the fold opens up, and this is a major feature distinguishing this antelope from gazelles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> Springbok differ from gazelles in several other ways; for instance, springbok have two <a href="/wiki/Premolar" title="Premolar">premolars</a> on both sides of either jaw, rather than the three observed in gazelles. This gives a total of 28 teeth in the springbok, rather than 32 of gazelles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> Other points of difference include a longer, broader and rigid bridge to the nose and more muscular cheeks in springbok, and differences in the structure of the horns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Both sexes have black horns, about 35–50 cm (14–20 in) long, that are straight at the base and then curve backward. In <i>A. m. marsupialis</i>, females have thinner horns than males; the horns of females are only 60 to 70 percent as long as those of males. Horns have a girth of 71–83 mm (2.8–3.3 in) at the base; this thins to 56–65 mm (2.2–2.6 in) towards the tip. In the other two subspecies, horns of both sexes are nearly similar. The <a href="/wiki/Spoor_(animal)" title="Spoor (animal)">spoor</a>, narrow and sharp, is 5.5 cm (2.2 in) long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_behaviour">Ecology and behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Ecology and behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herd_of_springboks.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2932" data-file-width="5184" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Herd_of_springboks.JPG/220px-Herd_of_springboks.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Herd_of_springboks.JPG/330px-Herd_of_springboks.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Herd_of_springboks.JPG/440px-Herd_of_springboks.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herd_of_springboks.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A herd</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Springbok are mainly active around dawn and dusk. Activity is influenced by weather; springbok can feed at night in hot weather, and at midday in colder months. They rest in the shade of trees or bushes, and often bed down in the open when it is cooler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skinner1996_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1996-16">[16]</a></sup> The social structure of the springbok is similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Thomson%27s_gazelle" title="Thomson's gazelle">Thomson's gazelle</a>. Mixed sex herds or <a href="/wiki/Harem_(zoology)" title="Harem (zoology)">harems</a> have a roughly 3:1 sex ratio; bachelor individuals are also observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1970_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1970-17">[17]</a></sup> In the mating season, males generally form herds and wander in search of mates. Females live with their offspring in herds, that very rarely include <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(ethology)" title="Dominance (ethology)">dominant</a> males. <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">Territorial</a> males round up female herds that enter their territories and keep out the bachelors; mothers and juveniles may gather in nursery herds separate from harem and bachelor herds. After weaning, female juveniles stay with their mothers until the birth of their next calves, while males join bachelor groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study of vigilance behaviour of herds revealed that individuals on the borders of herds tend to be more cautious; vigilance decreases with group size. Group size and distance from roads and bushes were found to have major influence on vigilance, more among the grazing springbok than among their browsing counterparts. Adults were found to be more vigilant than juveniles, and males more vigilant than females. Springbok passing through bushes tend to be more vulnerable to predator attacks as they can not be easily alerted, and predators usually conceal themselves in bushes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burger_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-burger-18">[18]</a></sup> Another study calculated that the time spent in vigilance by springbok on the edges of herds is roughly double that spent by those in the centre and the open. Springbok were found to be more cautious in the late morning than at dawn or in the afternoon, and more at night than in the daytime. Rates and methods of vigilance were found to vary with the aim of lowering risk from predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ritter2_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ritter2-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="1024" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg/220px-Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg/330px-Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg/440px-Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Locking_horns_-_Springbuck.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Springbok locking horns in a fight</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Rut_(mammalian_reproduction)" title="Rut (mammalian reproduction)">rut</a>, males establish territories, ranging from 10 to 70 hectares (25 to 173 acres),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-12"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> which they <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Territorial_marking" title="Territorial marking">mark</a> by urinating and depositing large piles of dung.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1972_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1972-4">[4]</a></sup> Males in neighbouring territories frequently fight for access to females, which they do by twisting and levering at each other with their horns, interspersed with stabbing attacks. Females roam the territories of different males. Outside of the rut, mixed sex herds can range from as few as three to as many as 180 individuals, while all-male bachelor herds are of typically no more than fifty individuals. Harem and nursery herds are much smaller, typically including no more than ten individuals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-13"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Springbok_pronk.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="361" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Springbok_pronk.jpg/220px-Springbok_pronk.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Springbok_pronk.jpg/330px-Springbok_pronk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/47/Springbok_pronk.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Springbok_pronk.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronking" title="Pronking">pronking</a> springbok</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In earlier times, when large populations of springbok roamed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalahari_desert" title="Kalahari desert">Kalahari desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karoo" title="Karoo">Karoo</a>, millions of migrating springbok formed herds hundreds of kilometres long that could take several days to pass a town.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> These mass treks, known as <i>trekbokken</i> in Afrikaans, took place during long periods of drought. Herds could efficiently retrace their path to their territories after long migrations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup> <i>Trekbokken</i> is still observed occasionally in <a href="/wiki/Botswana" title="Botswana">Botswana</a>, though on a much smaller scale than earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-estes-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-child_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-child-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>Springbok often go into bouts of repeated high leaps of up to 2 m (6 ft 7 in) into the air – a practice known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pronking" title="Pronking">pronking</a> (derived from the Afrikaans <i>pronk</i>, "to show off") or stotting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-14"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> In pronking, the springbok performs multiple leaps into the air in a stiff-legged posture, with the back bowed and the white flap lifted. When the male shows off his strength to attract a mate, or to ward off predators, he starts off in a stiff-legged trot, leaping into the air with an arched back every few paces and lifting the flap along his back. Lifting the flap causes the long white hairs under the tail to stand up in a conspicuous fan shape, which in turn emits a strong scent of sweat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1972_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1972-4">[4]</a></sup> Although the exact cause of this behaviour is unknown, springbok exhibit this activity when they are nervous or otherwise excited. The most accepted theory for pronking is that it is a method to raise alarm against a potential predator or confuse it, or to get a better view of a concealed predator; it may also be used for <a href="/wiki/Display_(zoology)" title="Display (zoology)">display</a>. Springbok are very <a href="/wiki/Fastest_animals" title="Fastest animals">fast</a> antelopes, clocked at 88 km/h (55 mph). They generally tend to be ignored by carnivores unless they are breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richard_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-richard-23">[23]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caracal" title="Caracal">Caracals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopards</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spotted_hyaena" title="Spotted hyaena">spotted hyaenas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wild_dog" title="Wild dog">wild dogs</a> are major predators of the springbok. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_wild_cat" title="African wild cat">African wild cats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black-backed_jackal" title="Black-backed jackal">black-backed jackals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_eagle" title="Black eagle">black eagles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martial_eagle" title="Martial eagle">martial eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tawny_eagle" title="Tawny eagle">tawny eagles</a> target juveniles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-15"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup> Springbok are generally quiet animals, though they may make occasional low-pitched bellows as a greeting and high-pitched snorts when alarmed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1972_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1972-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parasites">Parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2012 study scrutinised the effects of rainfall patterns and parasite infections on the body of the springbok in <a href="/wiki/Etosha_National_Park" title="Etosha National Park">Etosha National Park</a>. It was observed that males and juveniles were in better health toward the end of the rainy season. Health of females was more affected by parasites than by rainfall; parasite count in females peaked prior to and immediately after parturition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2012_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2012-24">[24]</a></sup> Studies show that springbok host <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helminth" title="Helminth">helminths</a> (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemonchus" title="Haemonchus">Haemonchus</a></i>, <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Longistrongylus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Longistrongylus (page does not exist)">Longistrongylus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Trichostrongylus" title="Trichostrongylus">Trichostrongylus</a></i>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ixodid" title="Ixodid">ixodid</a> <a href="/wiki/Tick" title="Tick">ticks</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Rhipicephalus" title="Rhipicephalus">Rhipicephalus</a></i> species), lice (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Damalinia&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Damalinia (page does not exist)">Damalinia</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Linognathus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Linognathus (page does not exist)">Linognathus</a></i> species).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Eimeria" title="Eimeria">Eimeria</a></i> species mainly affect juveniles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turner2012_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-Turner2012-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SPRINGBOK.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1932" data-file-width="2576" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/SPRINGBOK.jpg/220px-SPRINGBOK.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/SPRINGBOK.jpg/330px-SPRINGBOK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/SPRINGBOK.jpg/440px-SPRINGBOK.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SPRINGBOK.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Springbok feed on <a href="/wiki/Succulent_plant" title="Succulent plant">succulent grasses</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Springbok are primarily <a href="/wiki/Browsing_(herbivory)" title="Browsing (herbivory)">browsers</a> and may switch to grazing occasionally; they feed on shrubs and young <a href="/wiki/Succulent_plant" title="Succulent plant">succulents</a> (such as <i><a href="/wiki/Lampranthus" title="Lampranthus">Lampranthus</a></i> species) before they <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lignified" title="Lignified">lignify</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-east_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-east-27">[27]</a></sup> They prefer grasses such as <i><a href="/wiki/Themeda_triandra" title="Themeda triandra">Themeda triandra</a></i>. Springbok can meet their water needs from the food they eat, and are able to survive without drinking water through dry season. In extreme cases, they do not drink any water over the course of their lives. Springbok may accomplish this by selecting flowers, seeds, and leaves of shrubs before dawn, when the food items are most succulent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In places such as the Etosha National Park, springbok seek out water bodies where they are available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-east_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-east-27">[27]</a></sup> Springbok gather in the wet season and disperse during the dry season, unlike other African mammals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-east_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-east-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Springbok mate year-round, though females are more likely to enter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oestrus" title="Oestrus">oestrus</a> during the rainy season, when food is more plentiful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skinner1996_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1996-16">[16]</a></sup> Females are able to conceive at as early as six to seven months, whereas males do not attain <a href="/wiki/Sexual_maturity" title="Sexual maturity">sexual maturity</a> until two years;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rafferty_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-rafferty-5">[5]</a></sup> rut lasts 5 to 21 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup> When a female approaches a rutting male, the male holds his head and tail at level with the ground, lowers his horns and makes a loud grunting noise to attract her. The male then urinates and sniffs the female's <a href="/wiki/Perineum" title="Perineum">perineum</a>. If the female is receptive, she urinates as well, and the male makes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flehmen" title="Flehmen">flehmen</a> gesture, and taps his leg till the female leaves or permits him to mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1972_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1972-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-David1978_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-David1978-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gestation" title="Gestation">Gestation</a> lasts five to six months, after which a single calf (or rarely twins) is born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skinner1996_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1996-16">[16]</a></sup> Most births take place in the spring (October to November), prior to the onset of the rainy season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kingdon_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-kingdon-15">[15]</a></sup> The infant weighs 3.8 to 5 kg (8.4 to 11.0 lb); the female keeps her calf hidden in cover while she is away. Mother and calf rejoin the herd about three to four weeks after parturition; the young are weaned at five or six months. When the mother gives birth again, the previous offspring, now 6 to 12 months old, deserts her to join herds of adult springbok. Thus a female can calve twice a year, and even thrice if one calf dies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1972_4-5"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1972-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bigalke1970_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bigalke1970-17">[17]</a></sup> Springbok live for up to ten years in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cain2004_2-16"><a href="#cite_note-Cain2004-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Springbok_Distribution.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Springbok_Distribution.svg/220px-Springbok_Distribution.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Springbok_Distribution.svg/330px-Springbok_Distribution.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Springbok_Distribution.svg/440px-Springbok_Distribution.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Springbok_Distribution.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Range of springbok</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Springbok inhabit the dry areas of south and southwestern Africa. Their range extends from northwestern South Africa through the Kalahari desert into <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">Namibia</a> and Botswana. The <a href="/wiki/South_African_Republic" title="South African Republic">Transvaal</a> marks the eastern limit of the range, from where it extends westward to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> and northward to southern <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> and Botswana. In Botswana, they mostly occur in the Kalahari desert in the southwestern and central parts of the country. They are widespread across Namibia and the vast grasslands of the Free State and the shrublands of Karoo in South Africa; on the other hand, they are confined to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namib_desert" title="Namib desert">Namib desert</a> in Angola.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-estes_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-estes-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historic range of the springbok stretched across the dry grasslands, bushlands and shrublands of south-western and southern Africa; springbok migrated sporadically in southern parts of the range. These migrations are rarely seen nowadays, but seasonal congregations can still be observed in preferred areas of short vegetation, such as the Kalahari desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-east_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-east-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threats_and_conservation">Threats and conservation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Threats and conservation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The springbok has been classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Least_Concern" title="Least Concern">Least Concern</a> by the International Union for Conservation of Nature and Natural Resources (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IUCN" title="IUCN">IUCN</a>). There are no major threats to the long-term survival of the species. In fact, the springbok is one of the few antelope species with a positive population trend.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-east_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-east-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Springbok occur in several protected areas across their range: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Makgadikgadi_and_Nxai_National_Park" title="Makgadikgadi and Nxai National Park">Makgadikgadi and Nxai National Park</a> (Botswana); <a href="/wiki/Kgalagadi_Transfrontier_Park" title="Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park">Kgalagadi Transfrontier Park</a> between Botswana and South Africa; Etosha National Park and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namib-Naukluft_Park" title="Namib-Naukluft Park">Namib-Naukluft Park</a> (Namibia); <a href="/wiki/Mokala_National_Park" title="Mokala National Park">Mokala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karoo_National_Park" title="Karoo National Park">Karoo</a> National Parks and a number of provincial reserves in South Africa. In 1999, Rod East of the IUCN SSC Antelope Specialist Group estimated the springbok population in South Africa at more than 670,000, noting that it might be an underestimate. However, estimates for Namibia, Angola, Botswana, Transvaal, Karoo and the Free State (which gave a total population estimate of nearly 2,000,000 – 2,500,000 animals in southern Africa), were in complete disagreement with East's estimate. Springbok are under active management in several private lands. Small populations have been introduced into private lands and provincial areas of <a href="/wiki/KwaZulu-Natal" title="KwaZulu-Natal">KwaZulu-Natal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-east_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-east-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Springbok&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SouthAfrica-Stamp-1923-Springbok.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="237" data-file-width="279" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/SouthAfrica-Stamp-1923-Springbok.jpg/220px-SouthAfrica-Stamp-1923-Springbok.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/SouthAfrica-Stamp-1923-Springbok.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SouthAfrica-Stamp-1923-Springbok.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Postage stamp, South Africa, 1923</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Springbok are hunted as game throughout Namibia, Botswana and South Africa because of their attractive coats; they are common hunting targets due to their large numbers and the ease with which they can be supported on farmlands. The export of springbok skins, mainly from Namibia and South Africa, is a booming industry; these skins serve as <a href="/wiki/Taxidermy" title="Taxidermy">taxidermy</a> models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iucn_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">[1]</a></sup> The meat is a prized fare, and is readily available in South African supermarkets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Todorov_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Todorov-31">[31]</a></sup> As of 2011, the springbok, the <a href="/wiki/Gemsbok" title="Gemsbok">gemsbok</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greater_kudu" title="Greater kudu">greater kudu</a> collectively account for around two-thirds of the game meat production from Namibian farmlands; nearly 90 tonnes (89 long tons) of the springbok meat is exported as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanically_deboned_meat" title="Mechanically deboned meat">mechanically deboned meat</a> to overseas markets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2007 study indicated that the <a href="/wiki/Latissimus_dorsi_muscle" title="Latissimus dorsi muscle">latissimus dorsi muscle</a> of the springbok comprises 1.1–1.3% <a href="/wiki/Ash_(analytical_chemistry)" title="Ash (analytical chemistry)">ash</a>, 1.3–3.5% <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>, 72–75% moisture and 18–22% <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stearic_acid" title="Stearic acid">Stearic acid</a> is the main <a href="/wiki/Fatty_acid" title="Fatty acid">fatty acid</a>, accounting for 24–27% of the fatty acids. The <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> content varies from 54.5 to 59 milligrams (0.841 to 0.911 gr) per 100 grams (3.5 oz) of meat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> of the meat increases if the springbok is under stress or cropping is done improperly; consequently, the quality deteriorates and the colour darkens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2007_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2007-35">[35]</a></sup> The meat might be adversely affected if the animal is killed by shooting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The meat may be consumed raw or used in prepared dishes. <a href="/wiki/Biltong" title="Biltong">Biltong</a> can be prepared by preserving the raw meat with vinegar, spices and table salt, without <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a>, followed by drying. Springbok meat may also be utilised in preparing <a href="/wiki/Salami" title="Salami">salami</a>; a study found that the flavour of this salami is better than mutton salami, and feels oilier than salami of beef, horse meat or mutton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Todorov_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Todorov-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The springbok has been a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_symbol_of_South_Africa" title="National symbol of South Africa">national symbol of South Africa</a> since the <a href="/wiki/History_of_South_Africa#The_apartheid_era_1948.E2.80.931991" title="History of South Africa">white minority rule in the 20th century</a>. It was adopted as a nickname or <a href="/wiki/Mascot" title="Mascot">mascot</a> by several South African sports teams, most famously by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springbok_Rugby_Team" title="Springbok Rugby Team">national rugby union team</a>. The springbok is the national animal of South Africa. Even after the decline of apartheid, <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> intervened to keep the name of the animal for the reconciliation of rugby fans, the majority of whom were whites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ritter_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-ritter-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cap badge of <a href="/wiki/The_Royal_Canadian_Dragoons" title="The Royal Canadian Dragoons">The Royal Canadian Dragoons</a> has featured a springbok since 1913; a reference to the unit's involvement in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Boer_War" title="Second Boer War">Second Boer War</a>.</p>
